He specialized in molecular fungal biology, particularly of the species Colletotrichum. Dr. Katan joined the Volcani Center in 1993. His research interests focus primarily on the etiology, control, and fungus–host interactions of Colletotrichum species. His work is detailed in the publication "Colletotrichum: Biology, Pathology and Control," edited by J. A. Bailey and M. J. Jeger, with pages 88-120, published by CAB International, Wallingford, UK.
Dr. Shabi is a senior research scientist in the Department of Plant Pathology at the ARO. He received his B.S. and M.S. in agronomy in 1960 and his Ph.D. in plant pathology from the Hebrew University of Jerusalem in 1979. He joined the ARO in 1966 as a researcher focused on deciduous fruit, grape, and olive diseases. Dr. Shabi has spent sabbatical leaves at various institutions, including the University of Missouri, Columbia (1974-1975), the University of California, Davis, Cornell University (NYSAES, Geneva, NY, 1980), INRA, France, Lincoln University, New Zealand, and Clemson University, SC (1991-1992), and HRI, Wellesbourne, UK (1996-1997). His research has centered on disease control, epidemiology, and resistance of fungi to fungicides.
Dr. Bernstein's research interests include fungicide resistance in fungal pathogens affecting fruit trees, vegetables, and field crops. Her current focus is on vegetative compatibility grouping among populations of Fusarium oxysporum and Verticillium, as well as Colletotrichum on fruit trees, strawberries, and ornamentals.

Studies in filamentous ascomycetes have contributed significantly to mycology. In "Mycologia," Kanchana-udomkan et al. (2004) developed a bioassay to study anthracnose infection caused by Colletotrichum capsici in Capsicum chinense Jacq. Choi et al. (1999) focused on single spore isolation of fungi. Crous et al. (2009) compiled important findings in "Fungal Biodiversity," published in the CBS Laboratory Manual Series by the Centraalbureau voor Schimmelcultures, Utrecht, Netherlands.
Katoh and Standley (2013) introduced improvements in MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software (version 7), enhancing performance and usability for molecular biology research (Molecular Biology and Evolution 30: 772–780). Da Lio et al. (2017) reported the first occurrence of pear bitter rot caused by Colletotrichum fioriniae in France (Plant Disease 101: 1319).
Damm et al. (2013) described the Colletotrichum orbiculare species complex, which includes significant pathogens of field crops and weeds. Further studies by Damm et al. (2012) and Li et al. (2013) explored the Colletotrichum boninense and Colletotrichum fructicola species complexes, respectively, with Li et al. identifying the latter as responsible for bitter rot of pear in China (Plant Disease 97: 1000).
Lima et al. (2013) identified five Colletotrichum species associated with mango anthracnose in northeastern Brazil (Fungal Diversity 61: 75–88). Damm et al. (2014) investigated the Colletotrichum destructivum species complex, while Lin et al. (2002) conducted genetic analyses on resistance to pepper anthracnose caused by Colletotrichum capsici.
Further insights into the diversity of Colletotrichum species and their implications for plant biosecurity were presented by De Silva et al. (2017). Dean et al. (2012) highlighted the top ten fungal pathogens in molecular plant pathology, underscoring their significance.
Diao et al. (2017) documented Colletotrichum species causing anthracnose in chili peppers in China, while Douanla-Meli et al. (2018) conducted a multi-approach analysis of the diversity within Colletotrichum cliviae sensu lato.

Colletotrichum fructicola is identified as the predominant virulent species in strawberry plants in Chiba Prefecture, Japan. A new species, Colletotrichum miaoliense sp. nov., has also been described.
Corredor-Moreno P, Saunders DGO discuss the factors influencing the emergence of fungal and oomycete plant pathogens in their article.
Grammen A et al. conducted an identification and pathogenicity assessment of Colletotrichum isolates causing bitter rot of apple fruit in Belgium.
Dai FM and Ren XJ reported the first instance of anthracnose fruit rot of strawberry caused by Colletotrichum acutatum in China.
Gunnell PS and Gubler WD focused on the taxonomy and morphology of Colletotrichum species pathogenic to strawberry.
Damm U et al. explored the Colletotrichum acutatum species complex.
Gupta DR et al. reported the first case of anthracnose crown rot of strawberry caused by Colletotrichum siamense in Rajshahi District, Bangladesh.
Han YC et al. discussed the distribution and characteristics of Colletotrichum species associated with anthracnose of strawberry in Hubei, China.
Dean R et al. listed the top ten fungal pathogens in molecular plant pathology.
He L et al. characterized and assessed fungicide sensitivity of Colletotrichum species from various hosts in Shandong, China.
Hirayama Y et al. identified weeds as a potential inoculum source of Colletotrichum fructicola, responsible for strawberry anthracnose in Nara, Japan.
Denoyes-Rothan B et al. analyzed genetic diversity and pathogenic variability among isolates of Colletotrichum species from strawberry.
Howard CM discussed the black leaf spot phase of strawberry anthracnose caused by Colletotrichum.

The study of anthracnose fungi has been advanced through a combination of PCR and microtube hybridization (Jian et al., 2017). For instance, Jian Y, Li Y, Tang G, Zheng X, Khaskheli MI, and Gong G identified Colletotrichum species associated with anthracnose disease of strawberry in Sichuan Province, China (Plant Dis. 2021; 105(10): 3025-36). The findings can be accessed at https://doi.org/10.1094/PDIS-10-20-2114-RE.

Kar RK, Sharma N, and Verma UK discussed the presence of Colletotrichum in a Deccan intertrappean bed (Maastrichtian) in India (Cretaceous Research, 2004; 25(6): 945-50). For more details, visit https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cretres.2004.09.003.
Nirenberg HI, Feiler U, and Hagedorn G described Colletotrichum lupini in modern terms (Mycologia, 2002; 94: 307-20). The article can be accessed at https://doi.org/10.1080/15572536.2003.11833238.

This potential effector-triggered immunity might be repressed in this susceptible interaction. Moriwaki et al. (2009) announced a further genome project for Colletotrichum gloeosporioides. The project is a collaboration between CABI (CABI Europe-UK, Egham, UK) and The Genome Analysis Centre (TGAC), Norwich, UK (<tgac.bbsrc.ac.uk>), to carry out a whole genome sequence of the ex-epitype strain of C. gloeosporioides (IMI 356878). The DNA sequencing phase is nearing completion, and they are about to begin the assembly process.
The meeting provided substantial evidence of the rapid advancement in our understanding of Colletotrichum genomics. This progress was nicely complemented by outputs from another Colletotrichum workshop held earlier in 2010, in conjunction with the 10th European Conference on Fungal Genetics in Leeuwenhorst, the Netherlands. It was particularly exciting to witness the increasing power of genomic research tools and their potential impact on our understanding of fungal interactions.
Perfect et al. (1999) described Colletotrichum as a model genus for studies on pathology and fungal-plant interactions.
Tanaka et al. (2009) investigated how the Colletotrichum orbiculare ssd1 mutant enhances plant basal resistance through the activation of a mitogen-activated protein kinase pathway.
Phoulivong et al. (2010) noted that Colletotrichum gloeosporioides is not a common pathogen on tropical fruits.
Weir and Johnston (2010) characterized and neotypified Gloeosporium kaki Hori as Colletotrichum horii nom. nov.
These contributions reflect a diverse range of studies addressing the ecology and interactions of Colletotrichum, underscoring its significance in plant pathology.

Clark, F. A., R. L. Haaland, E. M. Gray, and D. M. Ball. 1980. Diseases of alfalfa in Alabama. Plant Disease 64:1015-1017.
A three-year survey of declining stands of alfalfa (Medicago sativa) in Alabama revealed several associated diseases. The main issues included crown rot caused by Sclerotinia trifoliorum and Rhizoctonia solani, along with summer anthracnose (Colletotrichum trifolii), black stem and leaf spot (Cercospora medicaginis), charcoal rot (Macrophomina phaseolina), and nematode infestations affecting seedling stands. Notably, rust (Uromyces striatus) and nematode crown rot were reported for the first time in the state. Of the 15 Rhizoctonia solani isolates collected, 11 were pathogenic on seedling alfalfa. Additionally, six other fungi and eight nematodes were linked to alfalfa health issues.
Historically, alfalfa production in Alabama peaked at 2,000 hectares in the early 1950s but drastically declined by the late 1950s due to the introduction of the alfalfa weevil (Hypera postica). However, since 1975, alfalfa acreage has steadily increased, with current estimates around 1,200 hectares, and plans for further expansion. Alfalfa is impacted by at least 70 different pathogens, with approximately 30 known to significantly limit its production.
Economic control of alfalfa diseases is typically achieved through plant resistance. An alfalfa breeding program is currently underway at Auburn University. Additionally, several private seed companies have expressed interest in developing alfalfa cultivars specifically for the Southern region. Identifying and assessing the major diseases affecting alfalfa in the state will not only assist in making informed selections of available varieties but will also provide essential information regarding the disease resistance that should be incorporated into cultivars developed for Alabama and its neighboring areas. The purpose of this paper is to update the information presented in Plant Disease, November 1980.

Table 4 presents the minimum, optimum, and maximum growth temperatures of Colletotrichum spp. The data includes five isolates of C. murrayae, five isolates of C. gloeosporioides, four isolates of C. fructicola, four isolates of C. aenigma, and three isolates of C. nymphaeae, all cultured on potato dextrose agar in the dark at varying temperatures for four days. The table shows the temperatures (°C) as follows: C. murrayae (10.0 a, 28.0 a, 38.8 b), C. gloeosporioides (10.0 a, 28.0 a, 40.0 a), C. fructicola (10.0 a, 28.4 a, 36.0 c), C. aenigma (10.0 a, 28.0 a, 36.0 c), C. nymphaeae (10.0 a, 28.0 a, 34.0 d). Means with the same letters are not significantly different (P ≤ 0.05). Table 5 outlines the pathogenicity of Colletotrichum isolates on strawberry, with data presented as mean ± standard deviation. The disease index was evaluated 20 days after inoculation, and the percentage of plant mortality was assessed 30 days post-inoculation. Mean percent mortality of strawberry due to conidial suspension of Colletotrichum spp. was measured by spray methods.

Abstract: This article discusses the first documented occurrence of Colletotrichum acutatum affecting strawberry plants in Malta. The research highlights the implications of this finding for local agriculture and plant pathology.

Colletotrichum niense is phylogenetically close to both subspecies of C. kahawae, but it is clearly differentiated based on multi-locus phylogenetic analyses. Thus, it represents a new species within the C. kahawae clade in the C. gloeosporioides species complex (Cannon et al., 2000).
Cannon, PF, Buddie AG, and Bridge PD (2008) provided insights into the typification of Colletotrichum gloeosporioides in their study published in Mycotaxon (104: 189–204). Additionally, C. hystricis, which is closely related to Citrus hystrix, was isolated from lesions cultivated on leaves and is known for its pleasant smell, being referred to as medicinal lime (Yaacob & Subhadrabandhu, 1995). While the fruit is not widely appreciated, it holds economic importance for the extraction of essential oils, often used in cooking and cosmetics (Allen, 1967).
Colletotrichum hystricis is distinct from C. alienum, which is commonly associated with cultivated fruits (Weir et al., 2012). In the present study, C. hystricis is described as a distinct taxon, supported by morphological differences such as the presence of obovoidal conidia and a slower growth rate in culture. Notably, C. alienum is characterized by the development of perithecia in culture, which the two strains of C. hystricis did not produce on artificial media in this study.
Various studies highlight the significance of Colletotrichum species in agriculture. Crouch et al. (2009) discussed anthracnose disease caused by the novel species Colletotrichum navitas, while Damm et al. (2013) addressed the Colletotrichum orbiculare species complex, which affects field crops and weeds. Other works by Damm et al. (2012) explored the Colletotrichum acutatum and Colletotrichum boninense species complexes.
Further research emphasizes the need for effective fungal identification to secure global food and fiber supplies (Crous et al., 2016a). Crous et al. have also contributed significantly to fungal biodiversity literature and the description of new fungal species in various journals, outlining the ongoing developments in mycology.

This version corrects typographical errors, adjusts formatting, and ensures consistency in citations. If you need any further modifications or details, let me know!
Crous PW, Groenewald JZ, Risède JM, Simoneau P, Hywel-Jones NL (2004) Noor NM, Zakaria L (2018) identified and characterized Colletotrichum and Calonectria species, as well as their Cylindrocladium anamorphs, which are associated with chili anthracnose in Peninsular Malaysia. Their findings were published in Studies in Mycology (50: 415-430) and the Journal of Plant Pathology (151: 961-973).
Crous PW, Verkleij GJM, Groenewald JZ, and Samson RA (2009) discussed fungal biodiversity. In the same context, Nylander JAA (2004) developed MrModeltest v. 2, a program distributed by the author. This was part of the CBS Laboratory Manual Series No. 1, published by the Centraalbureau voor Schimmelcultures at the Evolutionary Biology Centre, Uppsala University, Uppsala.
Utrecht Page RD (1996) introduced TREEVIEW, an application designed to display phylogenetic trees on personal computers, as noted in Computer Applications in the Biosciences (12: 357-358).
Damm U, Cannon PF, Woudenberg JH, Johnston PR, and Weir BS et al. (2012) examined the Colletotrichum boninense species complex in Studies in Mycology (73: 1-36). Prihastuti H, Cai L, Chen H, McKenzie EHC, and Hyde KD (2009) characterized Colletotrichum species associated with coffee berries in northern Thailand, as discussed in Fungal Diversity (39: 89-109).
Damm U, Sato T, Alizadeh A, Groenewald JZ, and Crous PW (2019) focused on the Colletotrichum species complexes of dracaenophilum, magnum, and orchidearum in Studies in Mycology (92: 1-46). Ranathunge NP and Hewa Bajjamage PS (2016) explored the deceptive behavior of Colletotrichum truncatum as an asymptomatic endophyte on non-host species, reported in the Journal of Plant Protection Research (56: 157-162).
Ranathunge NP, Mongkolporn O, Ford R, and Taylor PWJ (2012) studied the Colletotrichum truncatum pathosystem on Capsicum species, focusing on infection, colonization, and defense mechanisms, published in Australasian Plant Pathology (41: 463-473).
Rojas EI, Rehner SA, Samuels GJ, Van Bael SA, Herre EA et al. (2010) differentiated host-associated pathogens from asymptomatic endophytes in Colletotrichum gloeosporioides associated with Theobroma cacao and other plants in Panama, as shown in Mycologia (102: 1318-1338).
Ronquist F, Teslenko M, Mark P, Ayres DL, Darling A, and Höhna S (2012) presented MrBayes 3.2, an efficient Bayesian phylogenetic inference and model choice tool across a large model space, in Systematic Biology (61: 539-542).
Saini TJ, Gupta SG, Char BR, Zehr UB, and Anandalakshmi R (2016) reported the first instance of chili anthracnose caused by Colletotrichum karstii in India, noted in New Disease Reports (34: 6).
Sharma G, Kumar N, Weir BS, Hyde KD, and Shenoy BD (2013) found that the ApMat marker can resolve Colletotrichum species, as illustrated in a case study with Mangifera indica, documented in Fungal Diversity (61: 117-138).
Sharma G, Pinnaka AK, and Shenoy BD (2014) investigated the infra-specific diversity of Colletotrichum truncatum associated with chili anthracnose in India through microsatellite marker analysis, published in Archives of Phytopathology and Plant Protection (47: 2509-2523).
Sharma G and Shenoy BD (2014) reported on the involvement of Colletotrichum fructicola and C. siamense in chili anthracnose in India, also found in Archives of Phytopathology and Plant Protection (47: 1179-1194).
Shivas RG, Tan YP, Edwards J, Dinh Q, and Maxwell A et al. (2016) documented Colletotrichum species in Australia in Australasian Plant Pathology (45: 447-464).
Silva DN, Talhinhas P, Várzea V, Cai L, Paulo OS et al. (2012) applied the Apn2/MAT locus to improve the systematics of the Colletotrichum gloeosporioides complex, particularly with respect to coffee (Coffea spp.) hosts, as published in Mycologia (104: 396-409).
Silva JRA, Chaves TP, da Silva ARG, Barbosa LDF, and Costa JFO et al. (2017) conducted molecular and morpho-cultural characterization of Colletotrichum species associated with anthracnose on Capsicum spp. in northeastern Brazil, reported in Tropical Plant Pathology (42: 315-319).
Stephenson SA, Green JR, Manners JM, and Maclean DJ (1997) cloned and characterized glutamine synthetase from Colletotrichum gloeosporioides, demonstrating elevated expression during certain developmental stages.
Suwannarat, S., Steinkellner, S., Songkumarn, P., & Sangchote, S. (2017). Diversity of Colletotrichum spp. isolated from chili pepper fruit exhibiting symptoms of anthracnose in Thailand. Mycological Progress, 16, 677–686.
Swofford, D. L. (2003). PAUP*: Phylogenetic analysis using parsimony. Version 4.0 beta. Sinauer Associates, Sunderland.
Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A., & Kumar, S. (2013). MEGA6: Molecular evolutionary genetics analysis version 6.0. Molecular Biology and Evolution, 30, 2725–2729.
Than, P. P., Jeewon, R., Hyde, K. D., Pongsupasamit, S., Mongkolporn, O., & Taylor, P. W. J. (2008). Characterization and pathogenicity of Colletotrichum species associated with anthracnose on chili (Capsicum spp.) in Thailand. Plant Pathology, 57, 562–572.
Udayanga, D., Manamgoda, D. S., Liu, X., Chukeatirote, E., & Hyde, K. D. (2013). What are the common anthracnose pathogens of tropical fruits? Fungal Diversity.
Vieira, W. A. S., Michereff, S. J., De Morais, M. A. Jr., Hyde, K. D., & Câmara, M. P. S. (2014). Endophytic species of Colletotrichum associated with mango in northeastern Brazil. Fungal Diversity, 67, 181–202.
Voorrips, R. E., Finkers, R., Sanjaya, L., & Groenwold, R. (2004). QTL mapping of anthracnose (Colletotrichum spp.) resistance in a cross between Capsicum annuum and C. chinense. Theoretical and Applied Genetics, 109, 1275–1282.
Weir, B. S., Johnston, P. R., & Damm, U. (2012). The Colletotrichum gloeosporioides species complex. Studies in Mycology, 73, 115–180.

J., & White, T. J. (Eds.), PCR Protocols: A guide to methods and applications (pp. 315–322). Academic Press, San Diego.
Woudenberg, J. H. C., Aveskamp, M. M., de Gruyter, J., Spiers, A. G., & Crous, P. W. (2009). Multiple Didymella teleomorphs are linked to the Phoma clematidina morphotype. Persoonia, 22, 56–62.

The excerpt discusses the evaluation of commercial strawberry cultivars' resistance to anthracnose fruit rot caused by Colletotrichum nymphaeae under in vivo and greenhouse conditions. It highlights the prevalence of anthracnose fruit rot in Iran and assesses the susceptibility of 52 commercial cultivars against the pathogen. The results indicate varying resistance levels among the cultivars tested, with specific cultivars like 'Camarosa' showing notable sensitivity compared to others such as 'Gaviota' and 'Pajaro.' This study aims to provide insights into the resistance profiles of these cultivars against the anthracnose pathogen, contributing to better management strategies in strawberry cultivation.

The virulence diversity of anthracnose pathogens, specifically Colletotrichum acutatum and C. gloeosporioides species complexes, has been studied across eight olive cultivars commonly grown in Portugal. The work by Peres et al. (2005) highlighted the life cycles of C. acutatum, while Talhinhas et al. (2011) examined the epidemiology, histopathology, and aetiology of olive anthracnose caused by these pathogens. Perfect et al. (1999) identified Colletotrichum as a model genus for studies in pathology and fungal-plant interactions.
Prusky et al. (2013) discussed the quiescent and necrotrophic lifestyle choices of these fungi during postharvest disease development. They emphasized how these lifestyle choices influence disease outcomes. The distinct population structure of Colletotrichum species associated with olive anthracnose in the Algarve region reflects a complex host-pathogen relationship.
Further investigations by Queiroz et al. (2017) focused on molecular and phenotypic analyses that revealed associations of diverse Colletotrichum groups and a low level of C. gloeosporioides with olive anthracnose. In their work, they detailed the draft genome sequences of two isolates of Colletotrichum lindemuthianum, the causal agent of anthracnose in common beans.
Redman et al. (2001) explored the evolution of fungal symbiosis, discussing the transition from mutualism to parasitism and questioning who controls the outcome of this relationship—host or invader? Vargas et al. (2012) provided insights into plant defense mechanisms activated during both biotrophic and necrotrophic stages of Colletotrichum graminicola development in maize.
Additionally, Wharton and Diéguez-Uribeondo (2004) discussed the biology of Colletotrichum acutatum, contributing to the understanding of its role in plant pathology. Lastly, Monod et al. (2016) highlighted the highly conserved nature of certain genes across various species, indicating their potential importance in pathogenicity.
Sato, T., and Moriwaki, J. (2013). Reclassification of the Colletotrichum acutatum groups A2 and A4, and re-identification of strains belonging to these groups. Japan Plant Protection 67, 367–372. The activity of pyraclostrobin for the control of anthracnose fruit rot of strawberry, caused by Colletotrichum acutatum, is documented in Plant Disease, volume 90, pages 862-868.
Ureña-Padilla, A., Mitchell, D., and Legard, D. (2001) studied the oversummer survival of inoculum for Colletotrichum crown rot in buried strawberry crown tissue, published in Plant Disease, volume 85, pages 750-754.
Wang, N. Y., Forcelini, B. B., and Peres, N. A. (2018) conducted a multilocus sequence analysis that revealed Colletotrichum nymphaeae as the dominant species causing strawberry anthracnose in the United States. This research was abstracted in Phytopathology, volume 108, page S1.66.
Wang, N. Y., Turechek, W. W., and Peres, N. A. (2017) explored heat treatment as an alternative to fungicide application for the control of strawberry anthracnose caused by Colletotrichum acutatum, which is noted in the abstract of Phytopathology, volume 107, page S5.50.
Lastly, Ypema, H., and Gold, R. (1999) discussed the modification of a naturally occurring compound, kresoxim-methyl, to produce a new fungicide in Plant Disease, volume 83, pages 4-19.

Recent outbreaks of pepper anthracnose in Florida have raised concerns. The study by James et al. (2014) identified Colletotrichum siamense, C. theobromicola, and C. queenslandicum from several plant species, along with the identification of C. asianum in the Northern Territory of Australia (Australasian Plant Disease Notes 9:138).
Johnston and Jones (1997) explored the relationships among Colletotrichum isolates from fruit rots by assessing rDNA sequences (Mycologia 89:420-430). Kearse et al. (2012) introduced Geneious Basic, an integrated and extendable desktop software platform for the organization and analysis of sequence data (Bioinformatics 28:1647-1649). Kim et al. (1999) examined the differential interactions of a Colletotrichum gloeosporioides isolate with green and red pepper fruits (Phytoparasitica).
Larkin et al. (2007) provided updates on CLUSTALW and CLUSTALX, version 2.0 (Bioinformatics 23:2947-2948). Liu et al. (2016b) performed molecular and phenotypic characterization of Colletotrichum species associated with anthracnose disease in peppers from Sichuan Province, China (Scientific Reports 6:1-17). Liu et al. (2016a) discussed species boundaries in plant pathogenic fungi, using Colletotrichum as a case study (BMC Evolutionary Biology 16:1-14).
Liu et al. (2015) unraveled Colletotrichum species associated with Camellia by employing ApMat and gs loci to resolve species within the C. gloeosporioides complex (Persoonia 35). Manamgoda et al. (2013) identified an endophytic Colletotrichum species, C. endophytica, from tropical grasses (Fungal Diversity 61:107-115).
Alahakoon et al. (1994) studied the cross-infection potential of genetic groups of Colletotrichum gloeosporioides on tropical fruits (Physiological and Molecular Plant Pathology 44:93-103). Marin-Felix et al. (2017) provided insights into the genera of phytopathogenic fungi in their study, "GOPHY 1" (Studies in Mycology 86:99-216).
Auyong et al. (2015) investigated the role of cutinase and its impact on the pathogenicity of Colletotrichum truncatum (Journal of Plant Pathology and Microbiology 6:259-269). Mongkolporn et al. (2010) documented differential reactions on mature green and ripe chili fruit infected by three Colletotrichum species (Plant Disease 94:306-310). Mongkolporn and Taylor (2018) provided an overview of chili anthracnose, discussing Colletotrichum taxonomy and pathogenicity (Plant Pathology 67:1255-1263).
Carbone and Kohn (1999) proposed a method for designing primer sets for speciation studies in filamentous ascomycetes (Mycologia 91:553-556). Choi et al. (1999) focused on single spore isolation of fungi (Fungal Diversity 3:29-38). Crous et al. (2004b) established MycoBank, a comprehensive online resource for mycological taxonomic information.

Hu, M.-J., Grabke, A., and Schnabel, G. 2015. Investigation of the Colletotrichum gloeosporioides species complex causing anthracnose fruit rot of peach in South Carolina. Plant Disease 99:797-805.
